### Section: 12.1 Choice of Majors:

#### 12.1a Overview of Engineering Majors at MIT

The Massachusetts Institute of Technology (MIT) offers a wide range of engineering majors for students to choose from. These majors are designed to provide students with a strong foundation in the fundamentals of engineering, while also allowing them to specialize in a particular area of interest. In this section, we will provide an overview of the various engineering majors offered at MIT.

#### Aerospace Engineering

The Aerospace Engineering department at MIT is one of the oldest and most prestigious in the country. It offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of aerospace engineering, including aerodynamics, propulsion, and flight mechanics. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on cutting-edge research projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Biological Engineering

The Biological Engineering department at MIT is a relatively new field that combines principles from biology, engineering, and computer science. It focuses on the design and application of engineering principles to solve problems in the life sciences. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on interdisciplinary research projects and gain hands-on experience in labs and research facilities.

#### Chemical Engineering

The Chemical Engineering department at MIT is a highly competitive program that combines principles from chemistry, physics, and mathematics to design and optimize chemical processes. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on research projects in areas such as materials science, energy storage, and drug delivery.

#### Civil and Environmental Engineering

The Civil and Environmental Engineering department at MIT offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of civil and environmental engineering, including structural analysis, transportation, and environmental sustainability. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on real-world projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Computer Science and Engineering

The Computer Science and Engineering department at MIT is one of the largest and most prestigious in the country. It offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of computer science and engineering, including software design, algorithms, and data structures. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on cutting-edge research projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Electrical Engineering and Computer Science

The Electrical Engineering and Computer Science department at MIT is a joint department that combines principles from electrical engineering and computer science. It offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of these fields, including circuits, signals, and systems. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on cutting-edge research projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Materials Science and Engineering

The Materials Science and Engineering department at MIT is a multidisciplinary department that combines principles from materials science, chemistry, and physics. It offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of materials science and engineering, including crystal structures, mechanical properties, and surface chemistry. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on cutting-edge research projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Mechanical Engineering

The Mechanical Engineering department at MIT is a highly competitive program that combines principles from mechanics, thermodynamics, and materials science. It offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of mechanical engineering, including mechanics, thermodynamics, and design. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on real-world projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Nuclear Engineering

The Nuclear Engineering department at MIT is a highly competitive program that combines principles from physics, chemistry, and engineering to design and optimize nuclear systems. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on research projects in areas such as nuclear energy, radiation detection, and nuclear safety.

#### Ocean Engineering

The Ocean Engineering department at MIT is a multidisciplinary department that combines principles from civil engineering, mechanical engineering, and naval architecture. It offers a comprehensive curriculum that covers all aspects of ocean engineering, including offshore structures, marine vehicles, and ocean energy. Students in this major have the opportunity to work on cutting-edge research projects and collaborate with industry partners.

#### Other Engineering Majors

In addition to the majors listed above, MIT also offers a variety of other engineering majors, including Architecture, Aeronautics and Astronautics, and Urban Studies and Planning. These majors provide students with a unique opportunity to explore their interests and gain hands-on experience in a specific field.

In the next section, we will discuss the process of choosing a major at MIT and provide tips for students who are considering engineering as a career path.





### Section: 12.1b Choosing the Right Major

Choosing the right major is a crucial step in a student's academic journey. It is important for students to carefully consider their interests, goals, and strengths when making this decision. In this section, we will discuss some factors to consider when choosing a major at MIT.

#### Passion and Interest

One of the most important factors to consider when choosing a major is your passion and interest. What topics or subjects do you find yourself constantly drawn to? What do you enjoy learning about? These are important questions to ask yourself when considering a major. It is important to choose a major that aligns with your interests, as this will make your academic journey more enjoyable and fulfilling.

#### Career Goals

Another important factor to consider is your career goals. What do you want to do after graduation? What skills and knowledge do you need to achieve those goals? These are important questions to ask yourself when choosing a major. It is important to choose a major that will prepare you for your desired career path.

#### Strengths and Skills

It is also important to consider your strengths and skills when choosing a major. What are you good at? What skills do you excel in? These are important questions to ask yourself when considering a major. It is important to choose a major that aligns with your strengths and skills, as this will make your academic journey more manageable and successful.

#### Availability of Resources

Another important factor to consider is the availability of resources. Does MIT offer the specific major you are interested in? Are there enough resources and opportunities available for students in that major? These are important questions to ask yourself when choosing a major. It is important to choose a major that has enough resources and opportunities available for students.

#### Advice from Mentors and Advisors

Lastly, it is important to seek advice from mentors and advisors when choosing a major. They have a wealth of knowledge and experience and can provide valuable insights and guidance. It is important to listen to their advice and consider their recommendations when making your decision.

In conclusion, choosing the right major is a crucial step in a student's academic journey. It is important to consider your interests, goals, strengths, and available resources when making this decision. Seek advice from mentors and advisors and don't be afraid to explore different options before making a final decision. With careful consideration and guidance, you can choose a major that aligns with your passions and goals, and sets you on a successful academic and career path.





### Section: 12.1c Case Studies in Major Selection

In this section, we will explore some case studies of students who have successfully chosen their majors at MIT. These case studies will provide valuable insights and examples for students who are currently struggling with the decision of choosing a major.

#### Case Study 1: Choosing Aerospace Engineering

John, a high school student, always had a passion for space and technology. He was constantly reading about the latest advancements in space exploration and dreamed of one day working for NASA. When he was accepted into MIT, he knew that he wanted to pursue a major that would allow him to fulfill his dream. After researching the different majors offered at MIT, he decided to choose Aerospace Engineering.

John's decision was based on his passion for space and technology, as well as his interest in mathematics and physics. He also knew that MIT had a strong reputation for its Aerospace Engineering program and would provide him with the necessary skills and knowledge to achieve his career goals.

#### Case Study 2: Choosing Computer Science and Engineering

Sarah, a high school student, had always been interested in computers and technology. She took advanced computer science courses in high school and was constantly creating new programs and apps. When she was accepted into MIT, she knew that she wanted to pursue a major that would allow her to combine her passion for computers with her interest in engineering.

After researching the different majors offered at MIT, Sarah decided to choose Computer Science and Engineering. She knew that this major would provide her with the necessary skills and knowledge to pursue a career in software development or computer hardware design.

#### Case Study 3: Choosing Mechanical Engineering

Matt, a high school student, had always been interested in mechanics and design. He was constantly taking apart and rebuilding machines and was always curious about how things worked. When he was accepted into MIT, he knew that he wanted to pursue a major that would allow him to combine his passion for mechanics with his interest in engineering.

After researching the different majors offered at MIT, Matt decided to choose Mechanical Engineering. He knew that this major would provide him with the necessary skills and knowledge to pursue a career in mechanical design or robotics.

These case studies demonstrate the importance of considering one's interests, goals, and strengths when choosing a major. It is also important to research the different majors offered at MIT and seek advice from mentors and advisors. By carefully considering these factors, students can make informed decisions about their majors and set themselves up for success in their academic and career journeys.

These three areas are crucial in understanding the complex systems that make up our world and how they interact with each other. By studying these areas, we can gain a deeper understanding of the principles and concepts that govern them, and how they can be applied in engineering design.

We will begin by discussing the basics of engineering design and its importance in our society. We will then move on to explore the different types of engineering design, including mechanical, electrical, and civil engineering. Each of these areas will be discussed in detail, with examples and real-world applications to help us understand their significance.

Next, we will delve into the world of sea engineering, which involves the design and construction of ships, submarines, and other marine vessels. We will learn about the principles of hydrodynamics and how they are applied in sea engineering. We will also explore the challenges and advancements in this field, such as the development of autonomous underwater vehicles and the use of renewable energy sources in marine transportation.

Moving on to space engineering, we will discuss the design and construction of spacecraft and satellites. We will learn about the principles of aerodynamics and how they are applied in space engineering. We will also explore the challenges and advancements in this field, such as the development of reusable rockets and the use of 3D printing in spacecraft construction.

Finally, we will explore the world of earth engineering, which involves the design and construction of structures and systems that interact with the Earth's surface. We will learn about the principles of geotechnics and how they are applied in earth engineering. We will also explore the challenges and advancements in this field, such as the use of sustainable materials and the development of smart structures that can adapt to changing environmental conditions.

By the end of this chapter, we will have a solid understanding of the fundamentals of engineering design and how it applies to the three main areas of sea, space, and earth. We will also have gained insight into the challenges and advancements in these fields, and how they are shaping the future of engineering. So let's dive in and explore the exciting world of engineering design at MIT.

## Chapter 13: Engineering at MIT:




### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be exploring the fundamentals of engineering design through the lens of Prof. Steve Dubowsky, a renowned mechanical engineer from MIT. Prof. Dubowsky's expertise in the field of engineering design, particularly in the areas of sea, space, and earth, makes him an ideal guest lecturer for this chapter.

Prof. Dubowsky's research interests include the design and control of robotic systems, with a focus on underwater and space applications. His work has been instrumental in advancing the field of engineering design, particularly in the areas of autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs) and spacecraft.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into the various aspects of engineering design, as taught by Prof. Dubowsky. We will explore the principles and methodologies used in the design and control of robotic systems, with a particular emphasis on their application in the sea, space, and earth domains.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the fundamental concepts and techniques used in engineering design, as well as a deeper understanding of the practical applications of these concepts. We will also discuss the challenges and opportunities in the field of engineering design, and how these can be addressed through innovative and creative design solutions.

By the end of this chapter, readers will have a solid understanding of the fundamentals of engineering design, as well as the practical skills and knowledge necessary to apply these concepts in real-world engineering problems. This chapter will serve as a valuable resource for students, researchers, and professionals in the field of engineering design, providing them with the necessary tools and insights to excel in this exciting and rapidly evolving field.




#### 13.1a Introduction to Mobile Robots

Mobile robots are a fascinating and rapidly evolving field in engineering design. They are autonomous machines capable of locomotion, and they have become increasingly commonplace in commercial, industrial, and research settings. This section will provide an overview of mobile robots, including their components, capabilities, and applications.

#### 13.1a.1 Components of Mobile Robots

Mobile robots consist of several key components: a controller, sensors, actuators, and a power system. The controller is typically a microprocessor, embedded microcontroller, or personal computer (PC). It is responsible for processing sensor data, making decisions, and controlling the robot's actuators.

Sensors are a critical component of mobile robots. They provide the robot with information about its environment, allowing it to navigate and interact with its surroundings. The type of sensors used depends on the specific requirements of the robot. For example, a mobile robot designed for dead reckoning might use sensors such as accelerometers, gyroscopes, and odometers.

Actuators are the motors that move the robot. They can be wheeled or legged, depending on the type of locomotion the robot is designed for. The power system provides the necessary energy to operate the robot. This is typically a DC power system, but other systems can be used depending on the specific requirements of the robot.

#### 13.1a.2 Capabilities of Mobile Robots

Mobile robots have a wide range of capabilities, depending on their design and purpose. Some common capabilities include:

- Locomotion: Mobile robots can move around in their environment, either by rolling on wheels or by walking on legs.
- Navigation: Mobile robots can navigate their environment, either by following a predefined path or by using sensors to determine their location and orientation.
- Interaction: Mobile robots can interact with their environment and other objects, such as picking up and moving objects, or communicating with other robots or humans.
- Autonomy: Mobile robots can operate autonomously, without the need for physical or electro-mechanical guidance devices. This is known as autonomous mobile robot (AMR) technology.

#### 13.1a.3 Applications of Mobile Robots

Mobile robots have a wide range of applications in commercial, industrial, and research settings. Some common applications include:

- Material handling: Mobile robots are used in warehouses and factories to move materials and products, increasing efficiency and reducing labor costs.
- Exploration: Mobile robots are used in space and underwater environments to explore and collect data.
- Security: Mobile robots are used in security settings, such as patrolling large areas or detecting intruders.
- Research: Mobile robots are used in research labs to study and develop new technologies and algorithms.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the design and control of mobile robots, exploring topics such as kinematics, dynamics, and control algorithms. We will also discuss the challenges and opportunities in the field of mobile robotics, and how these can be addressed through innovative and creative design solutions.

#### 13.1b Designing Mobile Robots

Designing mobile robots is a complex process that requires a deep understanding of various engineering disciplines. It involves integrating the components of the robot, such as the controller, sensors, actuators, and power system, in a way that allows the robot to perform its intended tasks. This section will provide an overview of the design process for mobile robots.

#### 13.1b.1 Design Process

The design process for mobile robots typically follows a systematic approach. It begins with defining the requirements of the robot, including its capabilities, tasks, and environment. This is followed by selecting the appropriate components for the robot, taking into account factors such as cost, performance, and compatibility.

Once the components have been selected, they are integrated into the robot. This involves connecting the sensors to the controller, connecting the actuators to the power system, and ensuring that all components work together seamlessly. This process requires careful planning and testing to ensure that the robot functions as intended.

#### 13.1b.2 Design Considerations

Designing mobile robots involves considering a wide range of factors. Some of the key considerations include:

- Robot size and weight: The size and weight of the robot can affect its maneuverability, power requirements, and cost.
- Robot power system: The power system must be able to provide enough energy to operate the robot's components. This may involve using batteries, fuel cells, or other power sources.
- Robot sensors: The type and number of sensors used can affect the robot's ability to navigate and interact with its environment.
- Robot actuators: The type and number of actuators used can affect the robot's locomotion and interaction capabilities.
- Robot controller: The type and capabilities of the controller can affect the robot's decision-making and control capabilities.
- Robot cost: The cost of the robot's components and the design process must be considered.

#### 13.1b.3 Design Tools

Designing mobile robots can be aided by various tools and techniques. These include computer-aided design (CAD) software, which allows for the creation of detailed 3D models of the robot. Finite element analysis (FEA) can be used to simulate the behavior of the robot under various conditions, such as different loads or environments.

#### 13.1b.4 Design Challenges

Designing mobile robots presents several challenges. These include:

- Integration of components: Integrating the various components of the robot in a way that allows them to work together seamlessly can be a complex task.
- Robot reliability: Ensuring that the robot is reliable and can perform its tasks consistently can be a challenge.
- Robot cost: The cost of designing and building a mobile robot can be high, particularly for complex systems.
- Robot testing: Testing the robot's capabilities and performance can be a time-consuming and resource-intensive process.

Despite these challenges, the field of mobile robotics continues to advance rapidly, with new technologies and techniques being developed to overcome these challenges. As such, it remains an exciting and rewarding field for engineers to explore.

#### 13.1c Mobile Robots in Real World Applications

Mobile robots have found a wide range of applications in various industries, including manufacturing, healthcare, and transportation. This section will explore some of these real-world applications and how mobile robots are used in these contexts.

#### 13.1c.1 Manufacturing

In the manufacturing industry, mobile robots are used for a variety of tasks, including material handling, quality control, and assembly. For instance, in a factory setting, mobile robots can be used to move materials from one location to another, reducing the need for human labor and increasing efficiency. They can also be used for quality control, inspecting products for defects or anomalies. In addition, mobile robots can be used for assembly, assembling products according to a predetermined sequence of steps.

#### 13.1c.2 Healthcare

In the healthcare industry, mobile robots are used for tasks such as patient transport, medication delivery, and even surgery assistance. For example, in a hospital setting, mobile robots can be used to transport patients from one location to another, reducing the need for human assistance and improving patient comfort. They can also be used to deliver medication to patients, ensuring that patients receive the correct medication at the right time. In addition, mobile robots can be used for surgery assistance, performing tasks such as holding instruments or moving surgical tools.

#### 13.1c.3 Transportation

In the transportation industry, mobile robots are used for tasks such as cargo handling, security patrol, and even autonomous driving. For instance, in a port setting, mobile robots can be used to handle cargo, moving containers from one location to another. They can also be used for security patrol, patrolling large areas to detect intruders or anomalies. In addition, mobile robots can be used for autonomous driving, navigating through traffic and performing tasks such as delivery or inspection.

#### 13.1c.4 Other Applications

Mobile robots are also used in other industries, such as agriculture, energy, and space exploration. In agriculture, they can be used for tasks such as crop harvesting and pest control. In the energy industry, they can be used for pipeline inspection and maintenance. In space exploration, they can be used for tasks such as planetary exploration and satellite maintenance.

In conclusion, mobile robots have a wide range of applications in various industries. Their ability to perform tasks autonomously and efficiently makes them a valuable tool in many contexts. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more innovative applications of mobile robots in the future.




#### 13.1b Design and Construction of Mobile Robots

The design and construction of mobile robots is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors. This subsection will delve into the design and construction process, discussing the key considerations and techniques involved.

#### 13.1b.1 Design Considerations

When designing a mobile robot, several factors need to be considered. These include the robot's purpose, its environment, and the available resources. The robot's purpose will determine its capabilities and the type of sensors and actuators it needs. The environment in which the robot will operate will influence its locomotion system and power system. The available resources, including time, money, and expertise, will also impact the design process.

#### 13.1b.2 Construction Techniques

The construction of mobile robots can be done using various techniques. One common technique is the use of 3D printing, which allows for the rapid production of complex designs. Another technique is the use of modular components, which can be easily assembled and reconfigured for different applications.

#### 13.1b.3 Design Tools

Design tools play a crucial role in the design and construction of mobile robots. These tools can range from simple CAD software for creating 2D and 3D models to more advanced simulation tools for testing the robot's capabilities.

#### 13.1b.4 Design Process

The design process for mobile robots typically involves several iterations. The designer will start with a conceptual design, which is then refined through several iterations. Each iteration involves testing the design, making modifications, and repeating the process until the design meets the desired specifications.

#### 13.1b.5 Construction Process

The construction process for mobile robots can be done in a variety of ways. Some robots are assembled from pre-fabricated components, while others are built from scratch. The construction process can be done by the designer themselves or by a team of engineers.

#### 13.1b.6 Testing and Debugging

Once the robot is constructed, it needs to be tested and debugged. This involves testing the robot's capabilities, identifying and fixing any issues, and making further modifications if necessary.

#### 13.1b.7 Documentation

Documentation is an essential part of the design and construction process. It involves documenting the design process, the robot's capabilities, and any issues encountered during construction. This documentation can be used for future reference or to share with others.

In conclusion, the design and construction of mobile robots is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors. By understanding these factors and utilizing the appropriate design tools and techniques, engineers can create innovative and effective mobile robots for a variety of applications.

#### 13.1c Applications of Mobile Robots

Mobile robots have a wide range of applications in various fields, including industry, research, and education. This section will explore some of these applications, focusing on their use in factory automation infrastructure, research projects, and educational settings.

#### 13.1c.1 Factory Automation Infrastructure

Mobile robots are increasingly being used in factory automation infrastructure. They are particularly useful in tasks that require precise movement and navigation in complex environments. For example, they can be used to transport materials, perform quality control checks, and even assist in assembly processes. The use of mobile robots in factory automation can significantly improve efficiency and productivity, while also reducing the risk to human workers.

#### 13.1c.2 Research Projects

Mobile robots are also being used in a variety of research projects. For instance, the RoboBee project at MIT is using mobile robots to study the flight of insects. The project is developing a small, wireless robot that can mimic the flight of insects, which could have applications in areas such as environmental monitoring and search and rescue operations.

Another example is the use of mobile robots in the development of cellular models. These models, which simulate the behavior of cells and their interactions, can be used to study a variety of biological phenomena. Mobile robots can be used to create and manipulate these models, providing a powerful tool for researchers.

#### 13.1c.3 Educational Settings

Mobile robots are also being used in educational settings, both in primary and secondary schools, and in higher education. They provide a hands-on, interactive way for students to learn about engineering, computer science, and other related fields. For example, the Lego Mindstorms kit, which uses mobile robots, is a popular tool for introducing students to programming and robotics.

In higher education, mobile robots are being used in courses on robotics, control systems, and other related topics. They provide a practical, real-world context for students to learn and apply theoretical concepts.

#### 13.1c.4 Other Applications

Mobile robots have many other applications, including:

- Exploration of the sea: Mobile robots can be used to explore the sea, collecting data on ocean currents, temperature, and other parameters.
- Space exploration: Mobile robots can be used in space exploration, performing tasks such as navigation, inspection, and sample collection.
- Earthquake detection: Mobile robots can be used to detect and monitor earthquakes, providing real-time data on seismic activity.
- Disaster response: Mobile robots can be used in disaster response, assisting in search and rescue operations, delivering supplies, and monitoring the situation.

In conclusion, mobile robots have a wide range of applications, and their use is expected to grow in the future. As technology continues to advance, we can expect to see even more innovative uses for these versatile machines.




#### 13.1c Applications of Mobile Robots

Mobile robots have a wide range of applications in various fields, including medicine, scientific experimentation and exploration, and industrial automation.

#### 13.1c.1 Medicine

In the healthcare industry, mobile robots have several uses. They can be used for drug delivery, patient services, and other nursing functions. The small size of items typically carried around, which usually weigh less than 100 kg, allows for the use of robots much smaller than the MiR. Specialized equipment can be mounted on robots, allowing them to assist with surgical procedures. Their reliability and ability to reduce human error make them an important asset in the medical industry.

#### 13.1c.2 Scientific Experimentation and Exploration

In the scientific world, mobile robots have a large number of applications. Their ability to perform experiments and exploration without putting human lives in danger makes them an important asset. Unlike humans, robots do not require life support systems to function. In space travel, robots are performing science on planets and asteroids because sending humans is far more taxing on resources and money. The same is true in the oceanography domain. In fact, several of the same robotic systems are designed to perform their science under both conditions - space and underwater. In nuclear power plants, robots can service electronics and mechanical systems while reducing the risk to human workers.

#### 13.1c.3 Industrial Automation

Mobile industrial robots have many applications in the industrial sector. They are used in the food service industry, distribution applications, and home and industrial security. Their ability to navigate and perform tasks in these environments makes them an invaluable asset.

In conclusion, mobile robots have a wide range of applications in various fields. Their ability to perform tasks in a variety of environments, their reliability, and their ability to reduce human error make them an important asset in the modern world.




#### Conclusion

In this chapter, we had the privilege of learning from Prof. Steve Dubowsky, a renowned mechanical engineer from MIT. His expertise in the field of engineering design, particularly in the areas of sea, space, and earth, provided us with valuable insights and knowledge that will be instrumental in our future careers.

Prof. Dubowsky's lecture was a comprehensive exploration of the fundamentals of engineering design, with a particular focus on the challenges and opportunities presented by the sea, space, and earth. His lecture highlighted the importance of understanding the unique characteristics of these environments and how they impact the design process.

We learned about the importance of considering the sea as a dynamic and unpredictable environment, where design must account for factors such as wave action, currents, and salinity. We also explored the challenges of designing for space, where the extreme conditions of vacuum, temperature, and radiation must be carefully considered. Finally, we delved into the complexities of designing for the earth, where factors such as geography, climate, and sustainability must be taken into account.

Prof. Dubowsky's lecture also emphasized the importance of interdisciplinary collaboration in engineering design. The sea, space, and earth are complex systems that require expertise from a variety of fields, and Prof. Dubowsky's lecture underscored the importance of teamwork and communication in the design process.

In conclusion, Prof. Dubowsky's lecture was a valuable addition to our understanding of engineering design. His expertise and insights have provided us with a solid foundation for our future studies and careers in engineering.

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be exploring the fundamentals of engineering design through the lens of Prof. Sangbae Kim, a renowned mechanical engineer from MIT. Prof. Kim's expertise lies in the design and development of advanced robots, particularly those used in space exploration and underwater environments. His work has been instrumental in pushing the boundaries of what is possible in these fields, and his insights will provide a valuable perspective for our exploration of engineering design.

We will begin by discussing Prof. Kim's background and his journey to becoming a leading figure in the field of mechanical engineering. This will provide a foundation for understanding his approach to engineering design and how it has evolved over time. We will then delve into the specifics of his work, focusing on his contributions to the design of robots for space and underwater environments. This will include a detailed exploration of the challenges he has faced and the innovative solutions he has developed to overcome them.

Throughout the chapter, we will also be discussing the principles and methodologies that underpin Prof. Kim's approach to engineering design. This will include a focus on the role of interdisciplinary collaboration, the importance of understanding the constraints of the environment in which a system will operate, and the need for a systematic and iterative approach to design. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a deeper understanding of the fundamentals of engineering design and how they are applied in practice.




### Section: 14.1 Bioinspired Design:

Bioinspired design is a rapidly growing field that draws inspiration from nature to create innovative and efficient solutions to engineering problems. This approach is particularly relevant in the context of sea, space, and earth exploration, where the harsh and unpredictable environments pose unique challenges that can be addressed by studying and mimicking the strategies used by organisms that thrive in these environments.

#### 14.1a Basics of Bioinspired Design

Bioinspired design is a multidisciplinary field that combines principles from biology, engineering, and materials science. It involves studying the structures and behaviors of organisms, and then using this knowledge to design and develop new systems or products. The goal is to create solutions that are not only functional, but also sustainable and resilient, much like the organisms that inspire them.

One of the key principles of bioinspired design is biomimicry, which involves directly copying the structures or behaviors of organisms. For example, the Gherkin Tower in London, designed by Norman Foster, is a biomimetic structure that mimics the lattice-like exoskeleton and round shape of the Venus Flower Basket Sponge, a marine organism that lives in strong water currents. This design not only looks impressive, but also helps to disperse stresses on the building, much like the sponge disperses stresses in the water.

Another approach to bioinspired design is biomimetics, which involves using the principles behind the structures or behaviors of organisms to inspire new designs. For example, the Eastgate Centre in Harare, Zimbabwe, designed by architect Mick Pearce, is a large office and shopping complex that mimics the self-cooling mounds of African termites. The building has no air-conditioning or heating, but instead uses a passive cooling system inspired by the termites' self-cooling behavior. This approach not only reduces energy consumption, but also helps to minimize the building's environmental impact.

Bioinspired design also involves studying the materials used by organisms and then developing new materials or processes that mimic these properties. For example, the Qatar Cacti Building, designed by Bangkok-based Aesthetics Architects, is a projected building that uses the cactus's relationship to its environment as a model for building in the desert. The building is designed to collect and store water, much like a cactus, and to provide shade and protection from the sun, much like a cactus's spines.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these concepts and explore how they are applied in the field of bioinspired design. We will also discuss the challenges and opportunities associated with this approach, and how it can be used to address some of the most pressing issues facing sea, space, and earth exploration.

#### 14.1b Applications of Bioinspired Design

Bioinspired design has been applied in a wide range of fields, from architecture and urban planning to robotics and materials science. In this section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

##### Architecture and Urban Planning

As we have seen in the examples of the Gherkin Tower and the Eastgate Centre, bioinspired design has been used to create innovative and sustainable architectural structures. The Gherkin Tower, for instance, not only mimics the Venus Flower Basket Sponge in terms of its shape and structure, but also incorporates biomimetic principles in its design, such as the use of a lattice-like exoskeleton to disperse stresses. Similarly, the Eastgate Centre uses biomimetic principles to mimic the self-cooling behavior of African termites, resulting in a passive cooling system that reduces energy consumption.

In urban planning, bioinspired design has been used to create more sustainable and resilient cities. For example, the concept of the "sponge city" has been proposed, where cities are designed to mimic the water-absorbing and water-purifying capabilities of a sponge. This approach can help to mitigate the impacts of urbanization on water resources, and can also enhance the resilience of cities to extreme weather events.

##### Robotics

Bioinspired design has also been applied in the field of robotics, particularly in the development of biomimetic robots. These are robots that are designed to mimic the movements and behaviors of specific organisms. For example, the MIT Cheetah 3, developed by Prof. Sangbae Kim, is a biomimetic robot that mimics the movements of a cheetah, allowing it to run at high speeds and perform agile maneuvers. This technology has potential applications in a variety of fields, from search and rescue operations to military applications.

##### Materials Science

In materials science, bioinspired design has been used to develop new materials and processes that mimic the properties of biological materials. For example, researchers have been studying the properties of spider silk, which is one of the strongest and most durable natural materials known. By understanding the principles behind the production of spider silk, researchers have been able to develop new processes for producing synthetic spider silk, which could have a wide range of applications, from strengthening building materials to developing new types of medical devices.

In conclusion, bioinspired design is a powerful approach that can be used to address a wide range of engineering challenges. By studying and mimicking the strategies used by organisms, engineers can develop innovative and sustainable solutions that can help to address some of the most pressing issues facing sea, space, and earth exploration.

#### 14.1c Case Studies in Bioinspired Design

In this section, we will delve into some specific case studies that illustrate the application of bioinspired design in various fields. These case studies will provide a more detailed understanding of how bioinspired design can be used to solve complex engineering problems.

##### Case Study 1: The MIT Cheetah 3

The MIT Cheetah 3, developed by Prof. Sangbae Kim, is a prime example of bioinspired design in robotics. The Cheetah 3 is a biomimetic robot that mimics the movements of a cheetah, allowing it to run at high speeds and perform agile maneuvers. This technology has potential applications in a variety of fields, from search and rescue operations to military applications.

The design of the Cheetah 3 is heavily influenced by the biomechanics of a cheetah. The robot is designed to mimic the cheetah's unique running gait, which involves a series of bounding motions. This gait allows the cheetah to achieve high speeds while maintaining stability and agility. The Cheetah 3 replicates this gait using a series of pneumatic actuators that mimic the cheetah's muscles and joints.

The development of the Cheetah 3 also involves the use of advanced control algorithms that mimic the cheetah's nervous system. These algorithms allow the robot to make real-time adjustments to its movements based on sensory feedback, just like a cheetah. This level of control is crucial for the robot's ability to perform agile maneuvers at high speeds.

##### Case Study 2: The Sponge City Concept

The concept of the "sponge city" is another example of bioinspired design in urban planning. This concept, which has been proposed by researchers at MIT and other institutions, involves designing cities to mimic the water-absorbing and water-purifying capabilities of a sponge.

The sponge city concept is designed to address the challenges of urbanization, which often leads to increased water pollution and flooding. By mimicking the sponge's ability to absorb and purify water, the sponge city can help to mitigate these problems. For example, the city could be designed with permeable surfaces that allow rainwater to seep into the ground, reducing runoff and preventing flooding. The city could also incorporate green spaces that absorb and purify water, mimicking the sponge's water-purifying capabilities.

The sponge city concept also involves the use of advanced technologies, such as sensors and data analytics, to monitor and manage the city's water resources. These technologies can help to optimize the city's water management strategies, ensuring that water is used efficiently and sustainably.

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the power of bioinspired design in solving complex engineering problems. By studying and mimicking the strategies used by organisms, engineers can develop innovative and sustainable solutions that address the challenges of sea, space, and earth exploration.




### Section: 14.1b Techniques in Bioinspired Design

Bioinspired design is a multifaceted field that requires a variety of techniques to effectively mimic the structures and behaviors of organisms. These techniques can be broadly categorized into two types: direct and indirect.

#### Direct Techniques

Direct techniques involve directly copying the structures or behaviors of organisms. This can be achieved through biomimicry, where the exact structure or behavior of an organism is replicated. For example, the Gherkin Tower in London, designed by Norman Foster, is a biomimetic structure that mimics the lattice-like exoskeleton and round shape of the Venus Flower Basket Sponge, a marine organism that lives in strong water currents. This design not only looks impressive, but also helps to disperse stresses on the building, much like the sponge disperses stresses in the water.

Another direct technique is biomimetics, where the principles behind the structures or behaviors of organisms are used to inspire new designs. For example, the Eastgate Centre in Harare, Zimbabwe, designed by architect Mick Pearce, is a large office and shopping complex that mimics the self-cooling mounds of African termites. The building has no air-conditioning or heating, but instead uses a passive cooling system inspired by the termites' self-cooling behavior. This approach not only reduces energy consumption, but also helps to create a more sustainable and environmentally friendly building.

#### Indirect Techniques

Indirect techniques involve using the principles behind the structures or behaviors of organisms to inspire new designs, but without directly copying the organism. This can be achieved through bio-inspired design, where the principles behind the structures or behaviors of organisms are used to inspire new designs. For example, the design of the RoboBee, a small-scale robot developed at MIT, was inspired by the flight of insects. The researchers had to figure out how to build without rotary motors, gears, and nuts and bolts, which are not viable on such a small scale. In 2011, they developed a technique where they cut designs from flat sheets, layered them up, and folded the creation into shape. Glue was used to hold the folded parts together, analogous to the way insects use their exoskeleton for structural support.

In conclusion, bioinspired design is a rapidly growing field that offers a wealth of opportunities for engineers. By studying the structures and behaviors of organisms, engineers can develop innovative and efficient solutions to a wide range of problems. The techniques used in bioinspired design, both direct and indirect, provide a powerful toolkit for tackling these challenges.




### Subsection: 14.1c Case Studies in Bioinspired Design

In this section, we will explore some case studies that demonstrate the application of bioinspired design in various fields. These case studies will provide a deeper understanding of the principles and techniques discussed in the previous section.

#### Case Study 1: The Gherkin Tower

The Gherkin Tower, designed by Norman Foster, is a prime example of biomimicry in architecture. The tower's hexagonal skin is inspired by the Venus Flower Basket Sponge, a marine organism that lives in strong water currents. The lattice-like exoskeleton and round shape of the sponge help disperse stresses on the organism, and this principle is applied to the tower's design. The hexagonal skin of the tower helps to disperse the stresses on the building, much like the sponge disperses stresses in the water.

#### Case Study 2: The Eastgate Centre

The Eastgate Centre, designed by architect Mick Pearce, is a large office and shopping complex in Harare, Zimbabwe. The building's passive cooling system is inspired by the self-cooling mounds of African termites. The building has no air-conditioning or heating, but instead uses a passive cooling system that mimics the termites' self-cooling behavior. This approach not only reduces energy consumption, but also helps to create a more sustainable and environmentally friendly building.

#### Case Study 3: The RoboBee

The RoboBee, a small-scale robot developed at MIT, is an example of bio-inspired design. The design of the RoboBee was inspired by the flight of insects. The researchers had to figure out how to build a small, lightweight robot that could fly like an insect. This required a deep understanding of the principles behind insect flight and the application of these principles to the design of the RoboBee.

These case studies demonstrate the power and versatility of bioinspired design. By studying the structures and behaviors of organisms, engineers can develop innovative and effective solutions to a wide range of problems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of bioinspired design, a field that draws inspiration from nature to create innovative and efficient engineering solutions. We have learned about the principles of biomimicry and biomechanics, and how they can be applied to design and engineering problems. We have also had the privilege of learning from Prof. Sangbae Kim, a renowned expert in the field, who has shared his insights and experiences from his research and teaching at MIT.

Bioinspired design is a rapidly growing field, with potential applications in a wide range of areas, from robotics and materials science to energy systems and healthcare. As we continue to explore and understand the complexities of nature, we can expect to see even more exciting developments in this field.

In conclusion, bioinspired design is a powerful tool in the engineer's toolkit, offering a unique approach to problem-solving that is both innovative and sustainable. By learning from nature, we can create solutions that are not only efficient and effective, but also respectful of the natural world.

## Chapter: Exploring Sea, Space, & Earth: Fundamentals of Engineering Design

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will be exploring the fundamentals of engineering design in the context of sea, space, and earth. Engineering design is a systematic process that involves the application of scientific and mathematical principles to create solutions to real-world problems. It is a crucial aspect of modern society, as it is responsible for the design and development of a wide range of products and systems, from consumer electronics to spacecraft.

The field of engineering design is constantly evolving, and it is essential for engineers to stay updated with the latest developments and techniques. In this chapter, we will be focusing on the fundamentals of engineering design, which are the basic principles and processes that underpin all engineering design activities. These fundamentals are crucial for any engineer, regardless of their specialization or field of work.

We will begin by discussing the principles of engineering design, which are the fundamental concepts that guide the design process. These principles include problem identification, analysis, synthesis, and evaluation. We will also explore the different types of engineering design, such as product design, system design, and process design.

Next, we will delve into the processes of engineering design, which are the steps and methods used to apply the principles of engineering design. These processes include conceptual design, detailed design, and testing and evaluation. We will also discuss the role of computer-aided design (CAD) and other tools in the engineering design process.

Finally, we will explore the applications of engineering design in the context of sea, space, and earth. This will include examples of how engineering design is used in various industries, such as marine engineering, aerospace engineering, and environmental engineering. We will also discuss the challenges and opportunities that arise when applying engineering design in these fields.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of the fundamentals of engineering design and how they are applied in the context of sea, space, and earth. This knowledge will serve as a strong foundation for further exploration and study in the field of engineering design. So let's dive in and explore the exciting world of engineering design!

In this chapter, we had the privilege of learning from Prof. Sangbae Kim, a renowned expert in the field of mechanical engineering. His lecture on the fundamentals of engineering design provided us with a comprehensive understanding of the principles and processes involved in creating innovative and effective designs.

Prof. Kim's lecture highlighted the importance of a systematic and iterative approach to engineering design. He emphasized the need for a clear understanding of the problem at hand, followed by the application of design principles and processes. This approach not only ensures the creation of efficient and effective designs but also allows for continuous improvement and adaptation to changing requirements.

We also learned about the role of creativity and innovation in engineering design. Prof. Kim's lecture demonstrated how creativity can be harnessed to generate innovative solutions, and how these solutions can be evaluated and refined through a systematic process.

Furthermore, Prof. Kim's lecture provided us with a glimpse into the future of engineering design. He discussed the potential impact of emerging technologies such as artificial intelligence and nanotechnology on the field of engineering design. This not only piqued our interest but also highlighted the importance of staying abreast of the latest developments in the field.

In conclusion, Prof. Kim's lecture was a valuable addition to our understanding of engineering design. It provided us with a solid foundation in the principles and processes of engineering design, while also highlighting the importance of creativity, innovation, and staying abreast of the latest developments in the field.
